<p><b>James Earl</b> "<b>Jimmy</b>" <b>Carter, Jr.</b> (born October 1, 1924) is an American politician and author who served as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_the_United_States" title="List of Presidents of the United States">39th</a> <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a> from 1977 to 1981. He was awarded the 2002 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a> for his work with the <a href="/wiki/Carter_Center" title="Carter Center">Carter Center</a>.</p>
<p>Carter, a <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democrat</a> raised in rural <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a>, was a peanut farmer who served two terms as a <a href="/wiki/Georgia_Senate" title="Georgia Senate" class="mw-redirect">Georgia State Senator</a>, from 1963 to 1967, and one as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Governors_of_Georgia" title="List of Governors of Georgia">Governor of Georgia</a>, from 1971 to 1975. He <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1976" title="United States presidential election, 1976">was elected President in 1976</a>, defeating incumbent President <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">Gerald Ford</a> in a relatively close election, the Electoral College margin of 57 votes was the closest at that time since <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1916" title="United States presidential election, 1916">1916</a>.</p>
<p>On his second day in office, Carter pardoned all evaders of the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a> drafts. During his term as President, Carter created two new cabinet-level departments, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">Department of Energy</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Education" title="United States Department of Education">Department of Education</a>. He established a <a href="/wiki/Energy_policy_of_the_United_States" title="Energy policy of the United States">national energy policy</a> that included conservation, price control, and new technology. In foreign affairs, Carter pursued the <a href="/wiki/Camp_David_Accords" title="Camp David Accords">Camp David Accords</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Panama_Canal_Treaties" title="Panama Canal Treaties" class="mw-redirect">Panama Canal Treaties</a>, the second round of <a href="/wiki/Strategic_Arms_Limitation_Talks" title="Strategic Arms Limitation Talks">Strategic Arms Limitation Talks</a> (SALT II), and the return of the <a href="/wiki/Panama_Canal_Zone" title="Panama Canal Zone">Panama Canal Zone</a> to Panama. On the economic front he confronted persistent <a href="/wiki/Stagnation_and_inflation" title="Stagnation and inflation" class="mw-redirect">"stagflation"</a>, a combination of high inflation, high unemployment and slow growth. The end of his presidential tenure was marked by the 1979–1981 <a href="/wiki/Iran_hostage_crisis" title="Iran hostage crisis">Iran hostage crisis</a>, the <a href="/wiki/1979_energy_crisis" title="1979 energy crisis">1979 energy crisis</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Three_Mile_Island_accident" title="Three Mile Island accident">Three Mile Island nuclear accident</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_war_in_Afghanistan" title="Soviet war in Afghanistan" class="mw-redirect">Soviet invasion of Afghanistan</a>. In response to the Soviet move he ended détente, escalated the Cold War, and led the <a href="/wiki/1980_Summer_Olympics_boycott" title="1980 Summer Olympics boycott">international boycott of the 1980 Summer Olympics</a> in Moscow. By 1980, Carter's popularity had eroded such that, running for re-election that year, he ran against <a href="/wiki/Ted_Kennedy" title="Ted Kennedy">Ted Kennedy</a> in the Democratic Party's primaries for the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_presidential_primaries,_1980" title="Democratic Party presidential primaries, 1980">presidential nomination</a>, marking the most recent Democratic primary in which an incumbent faced opposition. Carter won the 1980 primary with a mere 51.13% of the vote (all incumbent candidates since have won at least 72.8% of their party's primary votes) but lost the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1980" title="United States presidential election, 1980">general election</a> in an electoral landslide to <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a> candidate <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a>, who won 44 of 50 states.</p>
<p>Although many consider him to have accomplished more with his post-presidency work, his presidency has drawn <a href="/wiki/Presidential_rankings" title="Presidential rankings" class="mw-redirect">medium-low responses</a> from historians. He set up the <a href="/wiki/Carter_Center" title="Carter Center">Carter Center</a> in 1982 as his base for advancing human rights. He has also traveled extensively to conduct peace negotiations, observe elections, and advance disease prevention and <a href="/wiki/Eradication_of_infectious_diseases" title="Eradication of infectious diseases">eradication</a> in developing nations. Additionally, Carter is a key figure in the <a href="/wiki/Habitat_for_Humanity" title="Habitat for Humanity">Habitat for Humanity</a> project. Regarding current political views, he has criticized of some of Israel's actions and policies in regards to the <a href="/wiki/Israeli%E2%80%93Palestinian_conflict" title="Israeli–Palestinian conflict">Israeli–Palestinian conflict</a>. He has vigorously opposed the Supreme Court's decision in <a href="/wiki/Citizens_United_v._FEC" title="Citizens United v. FEC">Citizens United v. FEC</a> to strike down <a href="/wiki/McCain-Feingold" title="McCain-Feingold" class="mw-redirect">McCain-Feingold</a> limits on campaign spending by corporations and unions, saying that America is "no longer a functioning democracy" and now has a system of "unlimited political <a href="/wiki/Bribery" title="Bribery">bribery</a>." He is a supporter of <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">President Obama</a> but has been critical of aspects of his foreign policy, particularly with regard to the <a href="/wiki/Drone_strikes_in_Pakistan" title="Drone strikes in Pakistan">use of drones</a> and Obama's decision not to close <a href="/wiki/Guantanamo_Bay_detention_camp" title="Guantanamo Bay detention camp">Guantanamo Bay detention camp</a>.</p>
<p>In August 2015, at age 90, Carter was diagnosed with <a href="/wiki/Melanoma" title="Melanoma">melanoma</a> which had metastasized to his liver and brain, and he began treatment which included surgery, immunotherapy, and radiation therapy.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jimmy_Carter_with_his_dog_Bozo_1937.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Jimmy_Carter_with_his_dog_Bozo_1937.gif/170px-Jimmy_Carter_with_his_dog_Bozo_1937.gif" width="170" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Jimmy_Carter_with_his_dog_Bozo_1937.gif/255px-Jimmy_Carter_with_his_dog_Bozo_1937.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Jimmy_Carter_with_his_dog_Bozo_1937.gif/340px-Jimmy_Carter_with_his_dog_Bozo_1937.gif 2x" data-file-width="452" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jimmy_Carter_with_his_dog_Bozo_1937.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Jimmy Carter (around age 13) with his dog, Bozo, in 1937</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Carter was born on October 1, 1924, at the Wise Sanatorium in <a href="/wiki/Plains,_Georgia" title="Plains, Georgia">Plains, Georgia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span>[</span>note 1<span>]</span></a></sup> He is a descendant of English immigrant Thomas Carter, who settled in Virginia in 1635. Numerous generations of Carters lived as cotton farmers in Georgia. Established in <a href="/wiki/Sumter_County,_Georgia" title="Sumter County, Georgia">Sumter County</a>, Plains was a <a href="/wiki/Boomtown" title="Boomtown">boomtown</a> of 600 people when Carter was born. Carter's father, <a href="/wiki/James_Earl_Carter,_Sr." title="James Earl Carter, Sr.">James Earl Carter, Sr.</a>, was a successful local businessman who ran a general store and had begun to invest in farmland. He had been a reserve second lieutenant in the U.S. Army's <a href="/wiki/Quartermaster_Corps_(United_States_Army)" title="Quartermaster Corps (United States Army)">Quartermaster Corps</a> during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>. Carter's mother, <a href="/wiki/Bessie_Lillian_Gordy" title="Bessie Lillian Gordy" class="mw-redirect">Bessie Lillian Gordy</a>, was a nurse at the Wise hospital. Carter was the first of Earl and Lillian's children; they moved several times during his infancy.<sup id="cite_ref-bourne-11-32_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bourne-11-32-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Carters settled on a dirt road in nearby Archery, which was almost entirely populated by impoverished <a href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">African American</a> families. They eventually had three more children—<a href="/wiki/Gloria_Carter_Spann" title="Gloria Carter Spann">Gloria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ruth_Carter_Stapleton" title="Ruth Carter Stapleton">Ruth</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Billy_Carter" title="Billy Carter">Billy</a>. Carter got along well with his parents, although his mother worked long hours and was often absent in his childhood. Although Earl was staunchly pro-segregation, he allowed his son to befriend the black farmhands' children. An enterprising teenager, Carter was given his own acre of Earl's farmland where he grew, packaged, and sold peanuts. Carter also rented out a section of tenant housing he had purchased.<sup id="cite_ref-bourne-11-32_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bourne-11-32-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Carter attended the Plains High School from 1930, first grade, to 1941. The <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression_in_the_United_States" title="Great Depression in the United States">Great Depression</a> had by then impoverished Archery and Plains, but the family benefited from <a href="/wiki/New_Deal" title="New Deal">New Deal</a> farming subsidies, and Earl took a position as a community leader. Young Carter was a diligent student with a fondness for reading.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span>[</span>note 2<span>]</span></a></sup> Carter's teacher, Julia Coleman, was an especially strong influence. As an adolescent, Carter played on the Plains High School basketball team; he also joined the <a href="/wiki/Future_Farmers_of_America" title="Future Farmers of America" class="mw-redirect">Future Farmers of America</a> and developed a lifelong interest in woodworking.<sup id="cite_ref-bourne-33-43_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bourne-33-43-4"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Naval_career">Naval career</span></h2>
<p>Carter had long dreamed of attending the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Naval_Academy" title="United States Naval Academy">U.S. Naval Academy</a> in <a href="/wiki/Annapolis,_Maryland" title="Annapolis, Maryland">Annapolis</a>. Meanwhile, he enrolled at <a href="/wiki/Georgia_Southwestern_College" title="Georgia Southwestern College" class="mw-redirect">Georgia Southwestern College</a> in nearby <a href="/wiki/Americus,_Georgia" title="Americus, Georgia">Americus</a>. After taking additional mathematics courses at <a href="/wiki/Georgia_Institute_of_Technology" title="Georgia Institute of Technology">Georgia Tech</a>, he was finally admitted to the Naval Academy in 1943. With his diminutive stature, Carter barely met the minimum physical requirements for entry. He was a good student but was seen as reserved and quiet, in contrast to the academy's culture of aggressive hazing of freshmen. While at the academy, Carter fell in love with his sister Ruth's friend Rosalynn Smith, whom he would marry shortly after his graduation in 1946.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> Carter graduated 60th out of 820 midshipmen in the class of 1946 and was commissioned as an <a href="/wiki/Ensign_(rank)" title="Ensign (rank)">ensign</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-annualRegister19461947_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-annualRegister19461947-7"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> From 1946 to 1953, Carter and Rosalynn lived temporarily in Virginia, Hawaii, Connecticut, and California, as he served deployments in the <a href="/wiki/US_Atlantic_Fleet" title="US Atlantic Fleet" class="mw-redirect">Atlantic</a> and <a href="/wiki/US_Pacific_Fleet" title="US Pacific Fleet" class="mw-redirect">Pacific fleets</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-zelizer-11_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zelizer-11-8"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Promoted to a <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant_junior_grade" title="Lieutenant junior grade" class="mw-redirect">LTJG</a>, he completed qualification for command of a diesel-electric submarine. In late 1952, he was admitted to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">US Navy's</a> fledgling <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_submarine" title="Nuclear submarine">nuclear submarine</a> program led by then-Captain <a href="/wiki/Hyman_G._Rickover" title="Hyman G. Rickover">Hyman G. Rickover</a>. Rickover's demands on his men and machines were legendary, and Carter later said that, next to his parents, Rickover was the greatest influence on his life.<sup id="cite_ref-bourne-72_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bourne-72-9"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On December 12, 1952, an accident with the experimental NRX reactor at <a href="/wiki/Atomic_Energy_of_Canada" title="Atomic Energy of Canada" class="mw-redirect">Atomic Energy of Canada</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Chalk_River_Laboratories" title="Chalk River Laboratories">Chalk River Laboratories</a> caused a partial meltdown. The resulting explosion caused millions of liters of radioactive water to flood the reactor building's basement, and the reactor's core was no longer usable.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> Carter was ordered to Chalk River to lead a U.S. maintenance crew that joined other American and Canadian service personnel to assist in the shutdown of the reactor.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> The painstaking process required each team member to don protective gear and be lowered individually into the reactor for a few minutes at a time, limiting their exposure to radioactivity while they disassembled the crippled reactor. During and after his presidency, Carter said that his experience at Chalk River had shaped his views on atomic energy and led him to cease development of a <a href="/wiki/Neutron_bomb" title="Neutron bomb">neutron bomb</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Carter took non-credit classes at <a href="/wiki/Union_College" title="Union College">Union College</a> in Schenectady, New York, in 1953.<sup id="cite_ref-zelizer-11_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zelizer-11-8"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup> Upon the death of his father that July, the family business became his, but deciding to leave Schenectady proved difficult. Rosalynn had also grown comfortable with their life. Returning to small-town life in Plains seemed "a monumental step backward," she said later. On the other hand, Carter felt restricted by the rigidity of the military and yearned to assume a path more like his father's. Thus resigning his commission, Carter was honorably discharged from the Navy on October 9, 1953.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Farming">Farming</span></h3>
<p>Earl Carter died a relatively wealthy man, having also recently been elected to the <a href="/wiki/Georgia_House_of_Representatives" title="Georgia House of Representatives">Georgia House of Representatives</a>. However, between his forgiveness of debts and the division of his wealth among heirs, his son Jimmy inherited comparatively little. For a year, Jimmy, Rosalynn, and their three sons lived in <a href="/wiki/Public_housing" title="Public housing">public housing</a> in Plains; Carter is the only U.S. president to have lived in housing subsidized for the poor. Knowledgeable in scientific and technological subjects, however, Carter set out to expand the family's peanut-growing business. The transition from Navy to agribusinessman was difficult, as the harvest his first year failed due to drought; Carter was compelled to open several bank lines of credit to keep the farm afloat. Meanwhile, he also took classes and read up on agriculture while Rosalynn learned accounting to manage the business's books. Though they barely broke even the first year, the Carters grew the business and became quite successful.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_political_career.2C_1963.E2.80.9371">Early political career, 1963–71</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Georgia_State_Senator_.281963.E2.80.9367.29">Georgia State Senator (1963–67)</span></h3>
<p>Racial tension was inflamed in Plains by the 1954 <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">U.S. Supreme Court</a>'s anti-segregation ruling in <i><a href="/wiki/Brown_v._Board_of_Education" title="Brown v. Board of Education">Brown v. Board of Education</a></i>. Carter was in favor of racial tolerance and integration—at one point, the local <a href="/wiki/Citizens%27_Councils" title="Citizens' Councils">White Citizens' Council</a> boycotted his peanut warehouse when he refused to join them—but he often kept those feelings to himself to avoid making enemies. By 1961 he was a prominent member of the community and the Baptist Church as well as chairman of the Sumter County school board, where he began to speak more loudly in favor of school integration.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Georgia_Senate" title="Georgia Senate" class="mw-redirect">state Senate</a> seat was opened by the dissolution of Georgia's <a href="/wiki/County_Unit_System" title="County Unit System">County Unit System</a> in 1962; Carter announced his run for the seat 15 days before the election. Rosalynn, who had an instinct for politics and organization, was instrumental to his campaign. The initial results showed Carter losing, but this was the result of fraudulent voting orchestrated by Joe Hurst, the sheriff of <a href="/wiki/Quitman_County,_Georgia" title="Quitman County, Georgia">Quitman County</a>. Carter challenged the results; when fraud was confirmed, a new election was held, which he won.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/African-American_Civil_Rights_Movement_(1954%E2%80%9368)" title="African-American Civil Rights Movement (1954–68)">Civil Rights Movement</a> was well underway as Carter took office. He and his family had become staunch <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> supporters. In 1962, the town of Americus was the site of mass beatings and incarcerations of black protesters, echoing similar unrest throughout the country. Carter remained relatively quiet on the issue at first, even as it polarized much of the county, to avoid alienating his segregationist colleagues. He did speak up on a few divisive issues, giving speeches against <a href="/wiki/Literacy_tests" title="Literacy tests" class="mw-redirect">literacy tests</a> and against a change to the Georgia Constitution which, he felt, implied a compulsion to practice religion.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup> A diligent legislator, Carter took speed-reading courses to keep up with the workload. Within two years his connections landed him on the state Democratic Executive Committee, where he helped rewrite the state party's rules. He became chairman of the West Central Georgia Planning and Development Commission, which oversaw the disbursement of federal and state grants for projects such as historic site restoration. When <a href="/wiki/Bo_Callaway" title="Bo Callaway">Bo Callaway</a> was elected to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">U.S. House of Representatives</a> in November 1964, Carter immediately began planning to unseat him. The two had previously clashed over which two-year college would be expanded to a four-year college program by the state; Carter wanted it to go to <a href="/wiki/Georgia_Southwestern_College" title="Georgia Southwestern College" class="mw-redirect">Georgia Southwestern College</a> in Americus, but Callaway wanted the funding to go to downtown <a href="/wiki/Columbus,_Georgia" title="Columbus, Georgia">Columbus</a>. Carter saw Callaway, a <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a>, as a rival who represented the inherited wealth and selfishness he despised in politics.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Carter was re-elected in 1964 to serve a second two-year term. For a time in the State Senate, he chaired its Education Committee; he also sat on the Appropriations Committee toward the end of his second term. Before his term ended he contributed to a bill expanding statewide education funding and getting Georgia Southwestern a four-year program. He leveraged his regional planning work, giving speeches around the district to make himself more visible to potential voters. The last day of the term, he announced his run for Congress.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1966_and_1970_campaigns_for_governor">1966 and 1970 campaigns for governor</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Georgia_gubernatorial_election,_1966" title="Georgia gubernatorial election, 1966">Georgia gubernatorial election, 1966</a> and <a href="/wiki/Georgia_gubernatorial_election,_1970" title="Georgia gubernatorial election, 1970">Georgia gubernatorial election, 1970</a></div>
<p>The congressional race was shaken up in mid-May when Callaway dropped out and decided to run for Governor of Georgia instead. Callaway was a very strong candidate, and state Democrats panicked over the prospect of losing the governorship they had held since <a href="/wiki/Reconstruction_Era" title="Reconstruction Era">Reconstruction</a>. Carter soon decided to follow Callaway and run for governor himself. In the Democratic primary he ran as a moderate alternative to both the liberal former governor <a href="/wiki/Ellis_Arnall" title="Ellis Arnall">Ellis Arnall</a> and the conservative <a href="/wiki/Lester_Maddox" title="Lester Maddox">Lester Maddox</a>. In a press conference he described his ideology as "Conservative, moderate, liberal and middle-of-the-road. ... I believe I am a more complicated person than that."<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> He lost the Democratic primary, but drew enough votes as a third-place candidate to force Arnall into a <a href="/wiki/Two-round_system" title="Two-round system">runoff election</a> with Maddox. A chain of events then resulted in Maddox, the <a href="/wiki/Dark_horse_candidate" title="Dark horse candidate" class="mw-redirect">dark horse candidate</a>, being elected governor.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>note 3<span>]</span></a></sup> The result was a sharp blow to Carter, who was left deeply in debt. His attempt to rescue the race from Callaway had resulted in the unlikely election of the segregationist Maddox, which he considered an even worse outcome.<sup id="cite_ref-bourne-153_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bourne-153-23"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Carter returned to his agriculture business and, during the next four years, carefully planned his next campaign for Governor in 1970. This period was a spiritual turning point for Carter; he grew increasingly evangelical, undertaking several religious missions in other states. Inspired by his sister Ruth and <a href="/wiki/Modern_liberalism_in_the_United_States" title="Modern liberalism in the United States">liberal</a> theologians such as <a href="/wiki/Reinhold_Niebuhr" title="Reinhold Niebuhr">Reinhold Niebuhr</a>, he declared himself <a href="/wiki/Born_again_(Christianity)" title="Born again (Christianity)">born again</a>, a growing movement in 1960s America. His last child <a href="/wiki/Amy_Carter" title="Amy Carter">Amy</a> was born around this time.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The liberal former governor, <a href="/wiki/Carl_Sanders" title="Carl Sanders">Carl Sanders</a>, was Carter's main opponent in the 1970 Democratic primary. Carter ran a more modern campaign this time around, employing printed graphics and statistical analysis. Responding to poll data, Carter leaned more <a href="/wiki/Conservatism_in_the_United_States" title="Conservatism in the United States">conservative</a> than before. He positioned himself as a <a href="/wiki/Populism" title="Populism">populist</a>, quickly going negative against Sanders for his wealth (labeling him "Cufflinks Carl") and associating him with the national Democratic Party. He accused Sanders of corruption, but when pressed by the media, could come up with no evidence.<sup id="cite_ref-bourne-180_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bourne-180-27"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hayward-46_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hayward-46-28"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> Throughout the campaign Carter sought both the black vote and the "Wallace vote", after the prominent segregationist <a href="/wiki/George_Wallace" title="George Wallace">George Wallace</a>. While he met with black figures such as <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King,_Sr." title="Martin Luther King, Sr.">Martin Luther King, Sr.</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Young" title="Andrew Young">Andrew Young</a>, and visited many black-owned businesses, he also praised Wallace and promised to invite him to give a speech in Georgia. He implied support or dislike of private schools depending on the audience. The appeal to racism became more blatant over time; Carter's senior campaign aides handed out a photograph of his opponent Sanders celebrating with black basketball players.<sup id="cite_ref-bourne-180_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bourne-180-27"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-hayward-46_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hayward-46-28"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>That September, Carter came ahead of Sanders in the first ballot by 49 to 38 percent, leading to a runoff. The campaign grew even more bitter; Carter's campaign criticized Sanders for supporting <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King,_Jr." title="Martin Luther King, Jr.">Martin Luther King, Jr.</a> Carter won the runoff election with 60 percent of the vote—winning 7 percent of the black vote—and went on to win the general election easily over the Republican <a href="/wiki/Hal_Suit" title="Hal Suit">Hal Suit</a>, a local news anchor. Once he was elected, Carter began to speak confidently against Georgia's racist politics. <a href="/wiki/Leroy_Johnson_(senator)" title="Leroy Johnson (senator)">Leroy Johnson</a>, a black state Senator, voiced his support for Carter, saying, "I understand why he ran that kind of ultra-conservative campaign. ... I don't believe you can win this state without being a racist."<sup id="cite_ref-bourne-180_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bourne-180-27"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Governor_of_Georgia_.281971.E2.80.9375.29">Governor of Georgia (1971–75)</span></h2>
<p>Carter was sworn in as the 76th Governor of Georgia on January 12, 1971. He declared in his inaugural speech that "the time of racial segregation was over. No poor, rural, weak, or black person should ever again have to bear the additional burden of being deprived of the opportunity for an education, a job, or simple justice." The crowd was reportedly shocked by this message, contrasting starkly with Georgia's political culture and particularly Carter's campaign. The many segregationists who had supported Carter during the race felt betrayed. <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> magazine ran a story on the <a href="/wiki/Progressivism_in_the_United_States" title="Progressivism in the United States">progressive</a> "<a href="/wiki/New_South" title="New South">New South</a>" governors elected that year in a May 1971 issue, featuring a cover illustration of Carter.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lester_Maddox" title="Lester Maddox">Lester Maddox</a>, Carter's predecessor as governor, became <a href="/wiki/Lieutenant_Governor_of_Georgia" title="Lieutenant Governor of Georgia" class="mw-redirect">lieutenant governor</a>. Carter had endorsed Maddox, although the two did not campaign as a <a href="/wiki/Ticket_(election)" title="Ticket (election)">ticket</a>. The two found little common ground during their four years of service, often publicly feuding with each other.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Richard_Russell,_Jr." title="Richard Russell, Jr.">Richard Russell, Jr.</a>, then <a href="/wiki/President_pro_tempore_of_the_United_States_Senate" title="President pro tempore of the United States Senate">President pro tempore of the United States Senate</a>, died in office during Carter's second week in office; the newly inaugurated governor appointed <a href="/wiki/David_H._Gambrell" title="David H. Gambrell">David H. Gambrell</a>, state Democratic Party chair, to fill Russell's unexpired term in the Senate.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>With Carter's reluctance to engage in back-slapping and political favors, the legislature found him frustrating to work with.<sup id="cite_ref-bourne-204_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bourne-204-34"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> He looked to aggressively expand the governor's authority while reducing the complexity of the state government. Therefore, he negotiated a bill allowing him to propose executive restructuring and to force a vote on it. He implemented <a href="/wiki/Zero-based_budgeting" title="Zero-based budgeting">zero-based budgeting</a> within state departments and added a Judicial Selection Commission to verify the credentials of judges appointed by the governor.<sup id="cite_ref-bourne-204_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bourne-204-34"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> The reorganization plan was submitted in January 1972, but had a cool reception in the legislature. After two weeks of negotiations it was passed at midnight on the last day of the session.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup> Ultimately he merged about 300 state agencies into 22—a fact he would emphasize in his presidential run—although it is disputed that there were any overall cost savings from doing so.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Civil rights were a heartfelt priority for Carter. He expanded the number of black state employees, judges, and board members. He hired Rita Jackson Samuels, a black woman, to advise him on potential appointments.<sup id="cite_ref-bourne-212_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bourne-212-38"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> He placed portraits of Martin Luther King, Jr., and two other prominent black Georgians in the capitol building, even as the <a href="/wiki/Ku_Klux_Klan" title="Ku Klux Klan">Ku Klux Klan</a> picketed the unveiling ceremony.<sup id="cite_ref-bourne-250_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bourne-250-39"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> Still, Carter tried to keep his conservative allies comfortable. He co-sponsored an anti-busing resolution with George Wallace at the 1971 National Governors Conference.<sup id="cite_ref-bourne-212_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bourne-212-38"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">U.S. Supreme Court</a> threw out Georgia's <a href="/wiki/Death_penalty_in_the_United_States" title="Death penalty in the United States" class="mw-redirect">death penalty</a> statute in <i><a href="/wiki/Furman_v._Georgia" title="Furman v. Georgia">Furman v. Georgia</a></i> (1972), Carter signed a revised death penalty statute which addressed the court's objections, thus re-introducing the practice in the state. Carter later regretted endorsing the death penalty, saying, "I didn't see the injustice of it as I do now."<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Carter pushed reforms through the legislature to provide equal state aid to schools in the wealthy and poor areas of Georgia, set up community centers for mentally handicapped children, and increased educational programs for convicts. He took pride in his program for the appointment of judges and state government officials. Under this program, all such appointments were based on merit, rather than political influence.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In one of his more controversial decisions,<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> he vetoed a plan to build a dam on Georgia's <a href="/wiki/Flint_River_(Georgia)" title="Flint River (Georgia)">Flint River</a>. After surveying the river and the literature himself, he argued that the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Army_Corps_of_Engineers" title="U.S. Army Corps of Engineers" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Army Corps of Engineers</a> was underestimating both the project's cost and its impact on the region. The veto won the attention of environmentalists nationwide.<sup id="cite_ref-bourne-250_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bourne-250-39"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup> When Lieutenant <a href="/wiki/William_Calley" title="William Calley">William Calley</a> was convicted in a military trial and sentenced to life for his role in the <a href="/wiki/My_Lai_Massacre" title="My Lai Massacre">My Lai Massacre</a> in <a href="/wiki/South_Vietnam" title="South Vietnam">South Vietnam</a>, a politically polarizing issue, Carter avoided paying direct tribute to Calley. He instead instituted "American Fighting Man's Day" and asked Georgians to drive for a week with their lights on in support of the military.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="National_ambition">National ambition</span></h3>
<p>Looking toward a potential presidential run, Carter engaged himself in national politics and public appearances. He was named to several southern planning commissions and was a delegate to the <a href="/wiki/1972_Democratic_National_Convention" title="1972 Democratic National Convention">1972 Democratic National Convention</a>, where the liberal U.S. Senator <a href="/wiki/George_McGovern" title="George McGovern">George McGovern</a> was the likely presidential nominee. Carter tried to ingratiate himself with the conservative, anti-McGovern voters, so that the convention would consider him for McGovern's running mate on a compromise ticket. He endorsed Senator <a href="/wiki/Henry_M._Jackson" title="Henry M. Jackson">Henry "Scoop" Jackson</a>, in part to distance himself from George Wallace. Carter was still fairly obscure at the time, and his attempt at triangulation failed; the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1972" title="United States presidential election, 1972">1972 Democratic ticket</a> went to McGovern and Senator <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Eagleton" title="Thomas Eagleton">Thomas Eagleton</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After McGovern's loss in November 1972, Carter began meeting regularly with his fledgling campaign staff. He had quietly decided to begin putting a presidential bid together. He tried unsuccessfully to become chairman of the <a href="/wiki/National_Governors_Association" title="National Governors Association">National Governors Association</a> to boost his visibility. On <a href="/wiki/David_Rockefeller" title="David Rockefeller">David Rockefeller</a>'s endorsement he was named to the <a href="/wiki/Trilateral_Commission" title="Trilateral Commission">Trilateral Commission</a> in April 1973. The following year he was named chairman of the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_National_Committee" title="Democratic National Committee">Democratic National Committee</a>'s congressional, as well as gubernatorial, campaigns.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1973 he appeared on the game show <i><a href="/wiki/What%27s_My_Line" title="What's My Line" class="mw-redirect">What's My Line</a></i>, where a group of celebrity panelists would try to guess his occupation. None recognized him and it took several rounds of question-and-answer before movie critic <a href="/wiki/Gene_Shalit" title="Gene Shalit">Gene Shalit</a> correctly guessed he was a governor.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1976_presidential_campaign">1976 presidential campaign</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)_presidential_primaries,_1976" title="Democratic Party (United States) presidential primaries, 1976" class="mw-redirect">Democratic Party (United States) presidential primaries, 1976</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1976" title="United States presidential election, 1976">United States presidential election, 1976</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1976.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/ElectoralCollege1976.svg/300px-ElectoralCollege1976.svg.png" width="300" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/ElectoralCollege1976.svg/450px-ElectoralCollege1976.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/ElectoralCollege1976.svg/600px-ElectoralCollege1976.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1004" data-file-height="588" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1976.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The electoral map of the 1976 election</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When Carter entered the Democratic Party presidential primaries in 1976, he was considered to have little chance against nationally better-known politicians; his <a href="/wiki/Name_recognition" title="Name recognition">name recognition</a> was two percent. As the <a href="/wiki/Watergate_scandal" title="Watergate scandal">Watergate scandal</a> of President Nixon was still fresh in the voters' minds, Carter's position as an outsider, distant from Washington, D.C., became an asset. He promoted government reorganization. Carter published <i>Why Not the Best?</i> in June 1976 to help introduce himself to the American public.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carter_and_Ford_in_a_debate,_September_23,_1976.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Carter_and_Ford_in_a_debate%2C_September_23%2C_1976.jpg/220px-Carter_and_Ford_in_a_debate%2C_September_23%2C_1976.jpg" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Carter_and_Ford_in_a_debate%2C_September_23%2C_1976.jpg/330px-Carter_and_Ford_in_a_debate%2C_September_23%2C_1976.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Carter_and_Ford_in_a_debate%2C_September_23%2C_1976.jpg/440px-Carter_and_Ford_in_a_debate%2C_September_23%2C_1976.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6000" data-file-height="3847" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carter_and_Ford_in_a_debate,_September_23,_1976.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Carter and President <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">Gerald Ford</a> debating at the <a href="/wiki/Walnut_Street_Theatre" title="Walnut Street Theatre">Walnut Street Theatre</a> in <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Carter became the front-runner early on by winning the <a href="/wiki/Iowa_caucus" title="Iowa caucus" class="mw-redirect">Iowa caucuses</a> and the <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire_primary" title="New Hampshire primary">New Hampshire primary</a>. He used a two-prong strategy: In the South, which most had tacitly conceded to Alabama's <a href="/wiki/George_Wallace" title="George Wallace">George Wallace</a>, Carter ran as a moderate favorite son. When Wallace proved to be a spent force, Carter swept the region. In the North, Carter appealed largely to conservative Christian and rural voters; he had little chance of winning a majority in most states. He won several Northern states by building the largest single bloc. Carter's strategy involved reaching a region before another candidate could extend influence there. He had traveled over 50,000 miles, visited 37 states, and delivered over 200 speeches before any other candidates announced that they were in the race.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> Initially dismissed as a regional candidate, Carter proved to be the only Democrat with a truly national strategy, and he clinched the nomination.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The national news media discovered and promoted Carter, as Lawrence Shoup noted in his 1980 book <i>The Carter Presidency and Beyond</i>:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>What Carter had that his opponents did not was the acceptance and support of elite sectors of the mass communications media. It was their favorable coverage of Carter and his campaign that gave him an edge, propelling him rocket-like to the top of the opinion polls. This helped Carter win key primary election victories, enabling him to rise from an obscure public figure to President-elect in the short space of 9 months.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Carter was interviewed by <a href="/wiki/Robert_Scheer" title="Robert Scheer">Robert Scheer</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Playboy" title="Playboy">Playboy</a></i> for the November 1976 issue, which hit the newsstands a couple of weeks before the election. While discussing his religion's view of pride, Carter said: "I've looked on a lot of women with lust. I've committed adultery in my heart many times."<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup> He is the only interviewee of <i>Playboy</i> to become US president.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As late as January 26, 1976, Carter was the first choice of only four percent of Democratic voters, according to a <a href="/wiki/Gallup_poll" title="Gallup poll" class="mw-redirect">Gallup poll</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Yet "by mid-March 1976 Carter was not only far ahead of the active contenders for the Democratic presidential nomination, he also led President <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">Ford</a> by a few percentage points", according to Shoup.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>He chose Senator <a href="/wiki/Walter_Mondale" title="Walter Mondale">Walter F. Mondale</a> as his running mate. He attacked Washington in his speeches, and offered a religious salve for the nation's wounds.<sup id="cite_ref-Dyer-2004_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dyer-2004-54"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Carter began the race with a sizable lead over Ford, who narrowed the gap during the campaign, but lost to Carter in a narrow defeat on November 2, 1976. Carter won the popular vote by 50.1 percent to 48.0 percent for Ford, and received 297 <a href="/wiki/Electoral_college" title="Electoral college">electoral votes</a> to Ford's 240. Carter became the first contender from the <a href="/wiki/Deep_South" title="Deep South">Deep South</a> to be elected President since the <a href="/wiki/US_presidential_election,_1848" title="US presidential election, 1848" class="mw-redirect">1848 election</a>. Carter carried fewer states than Ford—23 states to the defeated Ford's 27—yet Carter won with the largest percentage of the popular vote (50.1 percent) of any non-incumbent since <a href="/wiki/Dwight_Eisenhower" title="Dwight Eisenhower" class="mw-redirect">Dwight Eisenhower</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presidency_.281977.E2.80.9381.29">Presidency (1977–81)</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Carter_National_Portrait_Gallery.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/President_Carter_National_Portrait_Gallery.jpg/220px-President_Carter_National_Portrait_Gallery.jpg" width="220" height="360" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/President_Carter_National_Portrait_Gallery.jpg/330px-President_Carter_National_Portrait_Gallery.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e1/President_Carter_National_Portrait_Gallery.jpg 2x" data-file-width="367" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:President_Carter_National_Portrait_Gallery.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Robert Templeton's portrait of President Carter, displayed in the National Portrait Gallery, Washington DC</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carter-shah.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Carter-shah.jpg/220px-Carter-shah.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Carter-shah.jpg/330px-Carter-shah.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Carter-shah.jpg/440px-Carter-shah.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4256" data-file-height="2835" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carter-shah.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Jimmy Carter and <a href="/wiki/Mohammad_Reza_Pahlavi" title="Mohammad Reza Pahlavi">Mohammad Reza Pahlavi</a> in formal dinner in the <a href="/wiki/Niavaran_Palace" title="Niavaran Palace" class="mw-redirect">Niavaran Palace</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tehran" title="Tehran">Tehran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>. December 31, 1978.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Jimmy_Carter" title="Presidency of Jimmy Carter">Presidency of Jimmy Carter</a></div>
<p>Carter's tenure was a time of <a href="/wiki/Early_1980s_recession_in_the_United_States" title="Early 1980s recession in the United States">continuing inflation and recession</a>, as well as an <a href="/wiki/1979_energy_crisis" title="1979 energy crisis">energy crisis</a>. Among his first acts was the fulfillment of a campaign promise by issuing an <a href="/wiki/Executive_order_(United_States)" title="Executive order (United States)" class="mw-redirect">executive order</a> declaring unconditional <a href="/wiki/Amnesty" title="Amnesty">amnesty</a> for <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>-era <a href="/wiki/Draft_evasion" title="Draft evasion">draft evaders</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> On January 7, 1980, Carter signed <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.presidency.ucsb.edu/ws/index.php?pid=32978">Law H.R. 5860 aka Public Law 96-185</a> known as <i>The Chrysler Corporation Loan Guarantee Act of 1979,</i> bailing out <a href="/wiki/Chrysler_Corporation" title="Chrysler Corporation" class="mw-redirect">Chrysler Corporation</a>. He canceled military pay raises during a time of high inflation and government deficits.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Carter attempted to calm various conflicts around the world, most visibly in the Middle East with the signing of the <a href="/wiki/Camp_David_Accords" title="Camp David Accords">Camp David Accords</a>; giving back the <a href="/wiki/Panama_Canal" title="Panama Canal">Panama Canal</a>; and signing the <a href="/wiki/Strategic_Arms_Limitation_Talks#SALT_II" title="Strategic Arms Limitation Talks">SALT II nuclear arms reduction treaty</a> with Soviet leader <a href="/wiki/Leonid_Brezhnev" title="Leonid Brezhnev">Leonid Brezhnev</a>. His final year was marred by the <a href="/wiki/Iran_hostage_crisis" title="Iran hostage crisis">Iran hostage crisis</a>, which contributed to his losing the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1980" title="United States presidential election, 1980">1980 election</a> to <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> There is strong evidence that he supported and helped to instigate the <a href="/wiki/Iran%E2%80%93Iraq_War" title="Iran–Iraq War">Iran–Iraq War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Iran_hostage_crisis">Iran hostage crisis</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Iran_hostage_crisis" title="Iran hostage crisis">Iran hostage crisis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carter_Menorah.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Carter_Menorah.jpg/220px-Carter_Menorah.jpg" width="220" height="327" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3f/Carter_Menorah.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3f/Carter_Menorah.jpg 2x" data-file-width="266" data-file-height="395" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carter_Menorah.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
President Carter at first <a href="/wiki/National_Menorah" title="National Menorah">National Menorah</a> lighting in 1979.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On November 4, 1979 a group of Iranian students, belonging to the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_Student_Followers_of_the_Imam%27s_Line" title="Muslim Student Followers of the Imam's Line">Muslim Student Followers of the Imam's Line</a>, who were supporting the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_Revolution" title="Iranian Revolution">Iranian Revolution</a>, took over the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Embassy_in_Tehran" title="U.S. Embassy in Tehran" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Embassy in Tehran</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup> Fifty-two American diplomats and citizens were held hostage for the next 444 days until January 20, 1981. During the crisis, Carter remained in isolation in the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> for more than 100 days, until he left to participate in the lighting of the <a href="/wiki/National_Menorah" title="National Menorah">National Menorah</a> on the ellipse.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup> On April 24, 1980, Carter ordered <a href="/wiki/Operation_Eagle_Claw" title="Operation Eagle Claw">Operation Eagle Claw</a> to try free the hostages. The mission failed leaving eight American servicemen dead and the destruction of two aircraft.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._energy_crisis">U.S. energy crisis</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ShahCarter.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/ShahCarter.gif/220px-ShahCarter.gif" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/ShahCarter.gif/330px-ShahCarter.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/ShahCarter.gif/440px-ShahCarter.gif 2x" data-file-width="589" data-file-height="398" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ShahCarter.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Carter meeting with the <a href="/wiki/Shah" title="Shah">Shah</a> of Iran, <a href="/wiki/Mohammad-Reza_Pahlavi" title="Mohammad-Reza Pahlavi" class="mw-redirect">Mohammad-Reza Pahlavi</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Tehran" title="Tehran">Tehran</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On April 18, 1977, Carter delivered a televised speech declaring that the U.S. energy crisis during the 1970s was the <a href="/wiki/President_Jimmy_Carter%27s_Moral_Equivalent_of_War_Speech" title="President Jimmy Carter's Moral Equivalent of War Speech" class="mw-redirect">moral equivalent of war</a>. He encouraged energy conservation by all U.S. citizens and installed <a href="/wiki/White_House_solar_panels" title="White House solar panels" class="mw-redirect">solar water heating panels on the White House</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> He wore sweaters to offset turning down the heat in the White House.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Carter_DengXiaoping.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Carter_DengXiaoping.jpg/220px-Carter_DengXiaoping.jpg" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Carter_DengXiaoping.jpg/330px-Carter_DengXiaoping.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Carter_DengXiaoping.jpg/440px-Carter_DengXiaoping.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2946" data-file-height="1998" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Carter_DengXiaoping.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Carter meeting <a href="/wiki/Deng_Xiaoping" title="Deng Xiaoping">Deng Xiaoping</a>, leader of <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> from 1978 to 1992</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="EPA_Love_Canal_Superfund">EPA Love Canal Superfund</span></h3>
<p>In 1978, Carter declared a federal emergency in the neighborhood of <a href="/wiki/Love_Canal" title="Love Canal">Love Canal</a> in the city of <a href="/wiki/Niagara_Falls,_New_York" title="Niagara Falls, New York">Niagara Falls, New York</a>. More than 800 families were evacuated from the neighborhood, which was built on top of a <a href="/wiki/Toxic_waste" title="Toxic waste">toxic waste</a> landfill. The <a href="/wiki/Superfund" title="Superfund">Superfund</a> law was created in response to the situation.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup> Federal disaster money was appropriated to demolish the approximately 500 houses, the 99th Street School, and the 93rd Street School, which were built on top of the dump; and to remediate the dump and construct a containment area for the hazardous wastes. This was the first time that such a process had been undertaken. Carter acknowledged that several more "Love Canals" existed across the country, and that discovering such hazardous dumpsites was "one of the grimmest discoveries of our modern era".<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deregulation">Deregulation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AirlineDeregulationAct.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/AirlineDeregulationAct.png/220px-AirlineDeregulationAct.png" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/AirlineDeregulationAct.png/330px-AirlineDeregulationAct.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/AirlineDeregulationAct.png/440px-AirlineDeregulationAct.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="413" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AirlineDeregulationAct.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
U.S. President Jimmy Carter signs the <a href="/wiki/Airline_Deregulation_Act" title="Airline Deregulation Act">Airline Deregulation Act</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1977, Carter appointed <a href="/wiki/Alfred_E._Kahn" title="Alfred E. Kahn">Alfred E. Kahn</a>, a professor of <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a> at <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell University</a>, to be chair of the Civil Aeronautics Board (CAB). He was part of a push for deregulation of the industry, supported by leading economists, leading 'think tanks' in Washington, a civil society coalition advocating the reform (patterned on a coalition earlier developed for the truck-and-rail-reform efforts), the head of the regulatory agency, Senate leadership, the Carter administration, and even some in the airline industry. This coalition swiftly gained legislative results in 1978.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Airline_Deregulation_Act" title="Airline Deregulation Act">Airline Deregulation Act</a> (<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/pl-95-504">Pub.L. 95–504</a>) was signed into law by President Carter on October 24, 1978. The main purpose of the act was to <a href="/wiki/Airline_deregulation" title="Airline deregulation">remove government control</a> over fares, routes and market entry (of new airlines) from <a href="/wiki/Commercial_aviation" title="Commercial aviation">commercial aviation</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Civil_Aeronautics_Board" title="Civil Aeronautics Board">Civil Aeronautics Board</a>'s powers of regulation were to be phased out, eventually allowing market forces to determine routes and fares. The Act did not remove or diminish the FAA's regulatory powers over all aspects of airline safety.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In 1979, Carter deregulated the American beer industry by making it legal to sell <a href="/wiki/Malt" title="Malt">malt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hops" title="Hops">hops</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeast</a> to American <a href="/wiki/Homebrewing" title="Homebrewing">home brewers</a> for the first time since the effective 1920 beginning of <a href="/wiki/Prohibition_in_the_United_States" title="Prohibition in the United States">Prohibition in the United States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup> This Carter deregulation led to an increase in home brewing over the 1980s and 1990s that by the 2000s had developed into a strong craft <a href="/wiki/Microbrewery" title="Microbrewery">microbrew</a> culture in the United States, with 3,418 micro breweries, brewpubs, and regional craft breweries in the United States by the end of 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="U.S._boycott_of_the_Moscow_Olympics">U.S. boycott of the Moscow Olympics</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/1980_Summer_Olympics_boycott" title="1980 Summer Olympics boycott">1980 Summer Olympics boycott</a></div>
<p>In response to the <a href="/wiki/1979_Soviet_invasion_of_Afghanistan" title="1979 Soviet invasion of Afghanistan" class="mw-redirect">1979 Soviet invasion of Afghanistan</a>, Carter called for a boycott the <a href="/wiki/1980_Summer_Olympics" title="1980 Summer Olympics">1980 Summer Olympics</a> in Moscow, which raised a bitter controversy.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> It was the only time since the founding of the modern Olympics in 1896 that the United States had not participated in a Summer or Winter Olympics. The Soviet Union retaliated by boycotting the <a href="/wiki/1984_Summer_Olympics" title="1984 Summer Olympics">1984 Summer Olympics</a> in Los Angeles. It did not withdraw troops from Afghanistan until 1989 (eight years after Carter left office).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="1980_presidential_campaign">1980 presidential campaign</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1980" title="United States presidential election, 1980">United States presidential election, 1980</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1980.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/ElectoralCollege1980.svg/300px-ElectoralCollege1980.svg.png" width="300" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/ElectoralCollege1980.svg/450px-ElectoralCollege1980.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/ElectoralCollege1980.svg/600px-ElectoralCollege1980.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1004" data-file-height="588" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ElectoralCollege1980.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The electoral map of the 1980 election</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Carter later wrote that the most intense and mounting opposition to his policies came from the liberal wing of the Democratic Party, which he attributed to <a href="/wiki/Ted_Kennedy" title="Ted Kennedy">Ted Kennedy</a>'s ambition to replace him as president.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> Kennedy surprised his supporters by running a weak campaign, and Carter won most of the primaries and secured renomination. However, Kennedy had mobilized the liberal wing of the Democratic Party, which gave Carter weak support in the fall election.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1980" title="United States presidential election, 1980">Carter's campaign for re-election in 1980</a> was one of the most difficult, and least successful, in history. He faced strong challenges from the right (Republican <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a>), the center (independent <a href="/wiki/John_B._Anderson" title="John B. Anderson">John B. Anderson</a>), and the left (Democrat <a href="/wiki/Ted_Kennedy" title="Ted Kennedy">Ted Kennedy</a>). He had to run against his own "<a href="/wiki/Stagflation" title="Stagflation">stagflation</a>"-ridden economy, while the hostage crisis in Iran dominated the news every week. He alienated liberal college students, who were expected to be his base, by re-instating registration for the military draft. His campaign manager and former appointments secretary, <a href="/wiki/Timothy_Kraft" title="Timothy Kraft">Timothy Kraft</a>, stepped down some five weeks before the general election amid what turned out to have been an uncorroborated allegation of <a href="/wiki/Cocaine" title="Cocaine">cocaine</a> use.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> Carter was defeated by Ronald Reagan in a landslide, and the Senate went Republican for the first time since 1952.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-presidency_.281981.E2.80.93present.29">Post-presidency (1981–present)</span></h2>
<table class="metadata plainlinks ambox ambox-content ambox-BLP_sources" role="presentation">

</td>
