<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_opinion">Public opinion</span></h3>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/The_Independent" title="The Independent">The Independent</a></i> writes, "Carter is widely considered a better man than he was a president."<sup id="cite_ref-indy_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-indy-186"><span>[</span>183<span>]</span></a></sup> While he began his term with a 66&#160;percent approval rating,<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187"><span>[</span>184<span>]</span></a></sup> this had dropped to 34&#160;percent approval by the time he left office, with 55&#160;percent disapproving.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188"><span>[</span>185<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the wake of <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Nixon's</a> <a href="/wiki/Watergate_Scandal" title="Watergate Scandal" class="mw-redirect">Watergate Scandal</a>, exit polls from the 1976 Presidential election suggested that many still held <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Ford" title="Gerald Ford">Gerald Ford</a>'s pardon of Nixon against him.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189"><span>[</span>186<span>]</span></a></sup> By comparison Carter seemed a sincere, honest, and well-meaning Southerner.<sup id="cite_ref-indy_186-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-indy-186"><span>[</span>183<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1980 campaign, former California <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_California" title="Governor of California">Governor</a> <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> projected an easy self-confidence, in contrast to Carter's serious and introspective temperament. What many people believed to be Carter's personal attention to detail, his pessimistic attitude, his seeming indecisiveness and weakness with people were accentuated in contrast to Reagan's charismatic charm and delegation of tasks to subordinates.<sup id="cite_ref-profile_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-profile-190"><span>[</span>187<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nytimes_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes-191"><span>[</span>188<span>]</span></a></sup> Reagan used the economic problems, <a href="/wiki/Iran_hostage_crisis" title="Iran hostage crisis">Iran hostage crisis</a>, and lack of Washington cooperation to portray Carter as a weak and ineffectual leader. Carter was the first elected president since Hoover in 1932 to lose a reelection bid.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In the years since then, his reputation has much improved. Carter's presidential approval rating, at 31&#160;percent just prior to the 1980 election, was polled in early 2009 at 64 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192"><span>[</span>189<span>]</span></a></sup> His post-Presidency activities have been favorably received. Carter believes that <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">George H. W. Bush</a>, who actively sought him out and was far more courteous and interested in his advice than Reagan, contributed to the rise in his reputation.<sup id="cite_ref-indy_186-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-indy-186"><span>[</span>183<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h3>
<p>Carter's presidency was initially viewed by most as a failure.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193"><span>[</span>190<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194"><span>[</span>191<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195"><span>[</span>192<span>]</span></a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Historical_rankings_of_United_States_Presidents" title="Historical rankings of United States Presidents" class="mw-redirect">historical rankings of US presidents</a>, the Carter presidency has ranged from No. 19 to No. 34. Although his presidency received mixed reception, his peace keeping and humanitarian efforts since he left office have made Carter renowned as one of the most successful ex-Presidents in American history.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196"><span>[</span>193<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197"><span>[</span>194<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The documentary <i><a href="/wiki/Back_Door_Channels:_The_Price_of_Peace" title="Back Door Channels: The Price of Peace">Back Door Channels: The Price of Peace</a></i> (2009) credits Carter's efforts at Camp David, which brought peace between Israel and Egypt, with bringing the only meaningful peace to the Middle East. The film opened the 2009 Monte-Carlo Television Festival in an invitation-only royal screening<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198"><span>[</span>195<span>]</span></a></sup> on June 7, 2009 at the <a href="/wiki/Grimaldi_Forum" title="Grimaldi Forum">Grimaldi Forum</a> in the presence of <a href="/wiki/Albert_II,_Prince_of_Monaco" title="Albert II, Prince of Monaco">Albert II, Prince of Monaco</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199"><span>[</span>196<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Honors_and_awards">Honors and awards</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_honors_and_awards_received_by_Jimmy_Carter" title="List of honors and awards received by Jimmy Carter">List of honors and awards received by Jimmy Carter</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jimmy_Carter_with_model_of_SSN-23.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Jimmy_Carter_with_model_of_SSN-23.jpg/220px-Jimmy_Carter_with_model_of_SSN-23.jpg" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Jimmy_Carter_with_model_of_SSN-23.jpg/330px-Jimmy_Carter_with_model_of_SSN-23.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Jimmy_Carter_with_model_of_SSN-23.jpg/440px-Jimmy_Carter_with_model_of_SSN-23.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1056" data-file-height="693" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jimmy_Carter_with_model_of_SSN-23.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Former President and Navy submariner Jimmy Carter (left) hoists a replica of the <a href="/wiki/USS_Jimmy_Carter_(SSN-23)" title="USS Jimmy Carter (SSN-23)">USS Jimmy Carter (SSN-23)</a> given to him by Secretary of the Navy John H. Dalton (right) at a naming ceremony in the Pentagon on April 28, 1998</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Carter has received numerous awards and accolades since his presidency, and several institutions and locations have been named in his honor. His <a href="/wiki/Presidential_library" title="Presidential library">presidential library</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter_Library_and_Museum" title="Jimmy Carter Library and Museum">Jimmy Carter Library and Museum</a> was opened in 1986.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200"><span>[</span>197<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1998, the US Navy named the third and <a href="/wiki/USS_Jimmy_Carter_(SSN-23)" title="USS Jimmy Carter (SSN-23)">last <i>Seawolf</i>-class submarine</a> honoring former President Carter and his service as a submariner officer. It became one of the first US Navy vessels to be named for a person living at the time of naming.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201"><span>[</span>198<span>]</span></a></sup> That year he also received the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Prize_in_the_Field_of_Human_Rights" title="United Nations Prize in the Field of Human Rights">United Nations Human Rights Prize</a>, given in honor of human rights achievements,<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202"><span>[</span>199<span>]</span></a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Hoover_Medal" title="Hoover Medal">Hoover Medal</a>, recognizing engineers who have contributed to global causes.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203"><span>[</span>200<span>]</span></a></sup> He won the 2002 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Peace_Prize" title="Nobel Peace Prize">Nobel Peace Prize</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204"><span>[</span>201<span>]</span></a></sup> which was partially a response to President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">threats of war against Iraq</a> and Carter's criticism of the Bush administration.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205"><span>[</span>202<span>]</span></a></sup> Six of Carter's audiobook recordings have been nominated for the <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Award_for_Best_Spoken_Word_Album" title="Grammy Award for Best Spoken Word Album">Grammy Award for Best Spoken Word Album</a>; his book <i><a href="/wiki/Our_Endangered_Values" title="Our Endangered Values">Our Endangered Values</a></i> won the award in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206"><span>[</span>203<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207"><span>[</span>204<span>]</span></a></sup> The Souther Field Airport in Americus was renamed Jimmy Carter Regional Airport in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208"><span>[</span>205<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Carter was the first future U.S. president to be born in a hospital.<sup id="cite_ref-bourne-11-32_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bourne-11-32-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A popular anecdote holds that he was passed over for <a href="/wiki/Valedictorian" title="Valedictorian">valedictorian</a> after he and his friends skipped school to venture downtown in a <a href="/wiki/Hot_rod" title="Hot rod">hot rod</a>. Carter's truancy was mentioned in a local newspaper, although it is not clear he would have been valedictorian anyway.<sup id="cite_ref-bourne-33-43_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bourne-33-43-4"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-24"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-24">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">With Carter out of the race, Maddox narrowly won the runoff ballot over Arnall, clinching the Democratic nomination. In the general election, Callaway won a plurality of the vote but came short of the 50 percent majority. The election was thus decided by the Georgia House of Representatives with its Democratic majority; they settled on Maddox.<sup id="cite_ref-bourne-153_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bourne-153-23"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>

Retrieved <span class="nowrap">December 28,</span> 2014</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJimmy+Carter&amp;rft.btitle=JIMMY+CARTER+AND+THE+IRANIAN+HOSTAGE+CRISIS&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.whitehousehistory.org%2Fwhha_classroom%2Fclassroom_9-12-transitions-carter.html&amp;rft.pub=White+House+Historical+Association&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-58">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This information comes from a declassified "talking points" memo prepared by President Ronald Reagan's first Secretary of State, Alexander Haig, who in 1981 had just visited Egyptian leader Anwar Sadat and King Fahd of Saudi Arabia. Summing up the trip, which he found productive, Haig wrote: "Both Sadat and Fahd provided other bits of useful intelligence (e.g. Iran is receiving military spares for U.S. equipment from Israel). It was also interesting to confirm that President Carter gave the Iraqis a green light to launch the war against Iran through Fahd." See Blight, James. <i>Becoming Enemies: U.S.-Iran Relations and the Iran-Iraq War, 1979–1988</i>, Rowman &amp; Littlefield Publishers. May 3, 2012.</span></li>

"Recapturing leadership: The Carter administration and the crisis of confidence". <i>Presidential Studies Quarterly</i> <b>16</b> (3): 636–650.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AJimmy+Carter&amp;rft.atitle=Recapturing+leadership%3A+The+Carter+administration+and+the+crisis+of+confidence&amp;rft.aufirst=Robert+A.&amp;rft.aulast=Strong&amp;rft.date=Fall+1986&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.jtitle=Presidential+Studies+Quarterly&amp;rft.pages=636-650&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=16" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></li>
